GAME: Blue Moon City
RANK: 490
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Blue Moon City is one of those games (of which there are many) that makes a good first-impression with it's great production, easy-to-learn rules, quick game play, and oh-so-close ("I was one turn away!") finish. The problem is that these games aren't rewarding. I don't feel any satisfaction for outplaying my opponents, I don't get emotionally involved, I'm not given any interesting problem to solve, there's no excitement or drama, etc...  

As a wise man once said "You don't make friends with salad".

RATING: 6.5
Despite the very unusual theme (which almost prevented me from buying this), this is a typical quality Euro game.  Modular board, set collection, with a nice semi-cooperative element (similar to Tower of Babel, another Knizia), and a racing element, all combine to make a light strategy game that scales well from 2-4 players.

The goal of the game is to be the first player to provide a set number of crystals to the tower (the number of crystals depends on the number of players), hence the racing aspect.  On your turn you may move 1-3 spaces (the spaces consist of modular tiles that represent buildings that need to be reconstructed), make a contribution to a building (this is done by playing matching sets of cards), optionally play cards for their special abilities to augment your turn ( cards have two possible functions, there colour/strength and their special ability), and then finally you re-card netting 2 more cards.  There is a semi-cooperative element as it is very difficult to construct a building alone and you gain one time benefits for any building you have contributed to; the most optimal play is to help on buildings but to have helped the most as that player gains slightly more benefits.

Crystals, which are the winning currency of the game, are acquired through reconstruction of buildings, from previously adjacent reconstructed buildings, and indirectly from having dragons observe your reconstruction and them regarding you with dragon scales which get converted into crystals (yes the theme is VERY weird).  This games plays quickly, is easy to learn, is light but has some real decisions to made and plays great 2-4 players.

Have a home made version of the spielbox [boardgame=26189]expansion tiles[/boardgame], which are must have by the way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Update: Rating 6 -> 3

While the first few plays were fun, it became very clear how the game rams a tight endgame down your throat as long as you are playing with the basics in mind. After the last few games, the victor was only ahead by one turn. It seems like that should be exciting, but it felt arbitrary.

The way the tiles interrelate seems trivial, and the modular board doesn't really alter strategies, much like in Settlers of Catan (which also got old fast).

===========================

Certainly fun, but I think that'll wear out after about 10 plays or so. Despite the overdeveloped theme, it still feels incredibly dry. Card combos are fun to put together, but I imagine the appeal there is limited.

RATING: 7.5
A good (very) light middleweight, which I enjoy every time it comes out.  The alternate starting patterns available on BGG can jazz it up nicely.

Update: raised the score from 7.5 to 8 after a revisit a year or so on.  When everyone at the table knows the game well and you're playing random starting cities, this is a very good game.

Update: And back down to 7.5 after another run.  The BGG scale is a subjective measure, and as I play more games, I simply have more convenient options for a light middleweight.

RATING: 7
Blue Moon City is one of Knizia's more elegant designs. No funky math here. Very intuitive. And some awesome artwork with a whimsical sci-fi theme. 

Nevertheless, it is a bit bare bones in what you can do. I have no doubt that experts can really burn their brains at the decision making. But the elements involved within your decisions are not incredibly compelling to me. I find it merely a good hand management game with an interesting modular board that players compete over for area influence and other bonuses.

RATING: 6
At core this is a hand management game, in which you try to make the most efficient use of your cards (the special powers of many of the cards make this less straightforward than first appears), while discarding those that aren't essential.  Competition for the dragon scales and the time pressure of building the obelisk add interest.  Blue Moon City is not a deep game, but the mechanics are well integrated, the theming is nicely resonant of the card game, the artwork is fine, and it's fun to play occasionally.

I playtested this game.

RATING: 6
This game is just for experienced players.

The game is played without interaction between players that make who draw the best cards will be the guaranteed winner.

The colors of the game are very confusing for color-blind people but can be neglect for hard-core experienced player.

Examples of my words during play.
"My turn! What color is it and this and this?"
"OK! I will play these cards. Huh! wrong color of cards? Sorry, please wait for my new decision"
"My turn again! What color is it? I forgot"
"I will move Red Dragon 3 step with my red card. Cannot?, Why?"

RATING: 7
{2 plays, 4 player} My initial reaction was "wow," as I really liked everything going on in this game.  After my second play, however, I find myself slightly worried that you *must* follow the pack to have any chance, which is too bad if it's true, as I like the wide open board and how it initially allows you to maximize your hand, but later closes up and forces you to maybe spend a lot of cards.  Also, both games have felt just a bit too short, which I suppose is the better side to err on.  It's odd how some gameplay will "fit" its length, and this one has twice ended too soon for me, mostly, I think, because of the dragon scales adding more "money" - though I really like how the payoffs escalate as each adjacent region closes.

RATING: 8
Got fed up with not having a short, light 3 player game, so I finally went out and bought one.  Feels very overpriced for the components (especially if you bought it from the FLGS and didn't get 30% off).  Could easily have been a small box game, rather than overproduced big square box.  I just repacked it into a small Illuminati box with no problem, and I'm going to have to print a condensed version of the rules for easier carrying.

Nice theming.  The components are all very nicely produced, from the leaf-shaped dragon scales, to the plastic dragons, to the large tiles.  All of them are thick and sturdy.  The card deck is nicely coated as well.  Top notch components... but again, I would have settled for thinner, less pretty, and less expensive.

Gameplay is great.  Nice movement mechanics, combined with a reasonably deep resource management component.  It's a game where there's always something useful to do.

RATING: 7
An influence game that gives the feeling of a racing to an end.  It is mostly a card-driven game with a variable board layout (semi-random) to distribute influence locations.  Game play is clean and quick and offers choices, but does not facilitate long term planning over  several turns .  The use of the races from Blue Moon and giving each race an influence /ability reflective of how the decks are constructed is a nice tie-in, but overall the tie-ins are quite weak.  Art work is nicely done especially if you are a fan of the Blue Moon decks.  The game definitely feels like a Knizia design, but with more meat than some of his most recent card games.  One of his better designs in recent years. [GTI: TPCL]

RATING: 8
Most games have rules that place restrictions on your options, but this one seems to have pushed that approach straight off the highest tower it could find, and introduced an almost free-form modular smorgasbord of mechanisms, special action cards, and other turn enablers, and somehow keeping it straightforward. It plays like a breeze.

Several cards have an alternative use attached, and if you put your mind to it and have managed your hand well, the options available to you in a single turn offer a wide range of possible actions. Off course, if you are not satisfied with the usual 2 card redraw at the end of your turn, you can replace the dud cards in your hand too. Anything to be more effective in the next one. 

Now, on the heels of a wide range of possible actions, there usually lurks a substantial increase in thinking time per player, especially for a certain type of players. Instead, players seem quite capable to quickly settle on creative, and highly effective card usage without thinking all joy out of the experience for others. All the more impressive if you realize that certain cards allow players to turn up anywhere on the board. Some people appear to forget that they normally think through [i]all[/i] possible moves before committing.

Amazingly, it all falls into place quite pleasant. And astonishingly, it doesn't come unstuck at all, even when people get [i]very[/i] creative at combining card values with special powers and dragon calling. Someone did his homework - no Doktor needed to apply game-saving patches. It is well-balanced, and with a game like this, it suggests that some capable folk have put in some sound play-testing.

RATING: 7
I initially loved this game, because it is stylish and visually stunning, and also because you can use your cards in a variety of ways and therefore are never "stuck."  It's also cooperative -- the only competition is to reach the goals first, but you work together on mini-goals (rebuilding certain buildings).  There are no negative events that happen in the game, either played on each other or within the mechanics of the game, so it's really just a race.  Eventually that aspect is what makes the game less challenging.  Over time, you increase your efficiency, but there may not be a huge amount of strategy beyond that.  However, I happen to love game artwork and fun pieces (dragon scales, crystals, dragons, cubes, cards with neat artwork) so for me that alone makes Blue Moon City worth the price of admission.

RATING: 7
The last interesting thing Knizia has done--not his best game by any stretch of the imagination, but like many Knizia works it scales well, and the trick of having "weaker" cards having more powerful special effects is a clever one. Also, unlike many euros, you really have to pay attention to what everyone else is doing; the best Knizia games thrive on player interaction and this is not an exception.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I don't know what happened to Knizia, but before he went on hiatus he had a run of very good games replete with all the qualities we love in Knizia's games.  I am referring to Palazzo, Tower of Babel, Beowulf, and Blue Moon City.  Each game lies firmly in the middleweight category, attempts to keep all players engaged simultaneously and encourages some very direct player interaction; in short, the very best Euro game design has to offer.  The last of these and perhaps the weakest is Blue Moon City.  

I find Blue Moon City redolent of Tower of Babel in that players must strike a delicate balance between cooperation and competition.  The difference here is that that the cooperative element is a little more obliquely implemented and altogether too friendly.  Offerings in Tower of Babel always came with very mercenary and calculated intentions and the intricate and well-crafted scoring system keenly enhanced all the variables.  In Blue Moon City the benefits of cooperation requires that everyone work together and an atmosphere of peace, harmony and love usually reigns.  Admittedly, later in the game the assumed leader (usually,however, the game is too tight to finger anyone) has problems with acquiring building partners, but by that point it usually doesn't matter.

What I am trying to get at is that none of my decisions really seem to matter.  At the end of the game everyone has done almost equally well.  I can't remember a game without someone able to win on the same turn as the actual winner.  Tight games don't constitute a bad feature, but it just reinforces how I actually feel during the game.  I kind of coast along, following my cards and the other players, rarely making any truly important decisions and while I have a nice time, I play games for intellectual stimulation and Blue Moon City rarely does any of that.

RATING: 6
Okay efficiency game.  Feels abstract to me.  The game engine rewards players who find themselves in the right place at the right time... and rewards players that seed the board with their own cubes.  Although it's tempting to run off and work alone on the opposite side of the board, you really need to play "follow the leader" to get the most crystals and dragon scales (and to be a contendor for victory).  When one tile is completed, players who worked on the tile get goodies (and the biggest contributor gets the most goodies)... but then the tile is turned over and it adds a bonus (reward) to any adjacent tiles that get completed.  If you can't get the right cards to stay near your opponents on the profitable side of the board, you might find yourself at the back of the pack in a hurry.  It takes a long time to complete tiles on your own, so think twice before breaking ground on tiles that are far from your opponents.  Undoubtedly, efficiency is very important (both in card play and board play).  I like the race element created by the early "bricks" of the obelisk costing a lot less than later bricks... but I dislike the "follow the leader" element caused by the game's economic engine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5

RATING: 7
In our first 2-player "learning" game there was a clear lead for one player at the beginning, but the endgame was very close - whoever made it back to the obelisk first was the winner. The simple decision of one player NOT discarding some cards to draw more than two at the end of the anti-penultimate turn ended up being the game loser! My hope is that play with 3-4 will feel like less of a race for the finish line and more as though mid-game decisions matter.

Second play over a year later with 4 people, and nearly the same situation: very close endgame with a sudden race by what we thought was only two people, and then a single completed city changed the situation so that the others had more crystals, the obelisk filled up in less than a round, and a third person took the game with a surprising win from behind. Overall enjoyable, but not particularly challenging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players move about a razed city in Knizia's Blue Moon universe in order to reconstruct it. Buildings are reconstructed by making a series of "contributions" by playing cards in different suits (corresponding to the races found in said universe) and receiving rewards on the successful reconstruction of each building. The rewards can either be "crystals," "scales" (pieces which make up a separate sub-game which garners yet more crystals), cards or a combination of the three.

The cards work together in specific colours (suits) and also can have an "event card" effect which will be familiar to players of GMT's card-driven games. Amassing crystals allows the player to make further "contributions" to a central monolith which effectively acts like a VP track. First player to make a given number of escalating crystal contributions wins.

Play is simple and fast moving yet satisfying. While only for four players (so far), it makes a great filler for those nights when you still have a little time left but prefer slightly meatier fare than most fillers provide.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Players: 2-4 [2,(3,4)] (162 Votes)

RATING: 7.5
The game is easy enough to learn in 10 minutes, but requires so many decisions that it never seems simplistic. It plays in less than an hour, has a very cool theme, and has great artwork and components.

The luck factor is balanced nicely by the special effects of the cards. This means you have to make a lot of hard choices: do you use that grey card to move now, or use it in combination with a white card to build next turn? The addition of the dragon scales also forces you to decide whether to save cards or use them immediately for a shot at extra points. Even the fact that you have only ten cubes really changes the way you play, preventing you from spreading yourself too thin. I look forward to using the alternative tile layouts posted on BGG to make it more challenging.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a really clever game. When explaining the rules, it's actually pretty easy to make Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage jokes, because players are playing cards either for the values, 1-3, or for events, and it does actually have a touch of the flavor of Hannibal. Like most of Knizia's recent games, Blue Moon City eludes easy classification, having elements of auction, area-control, resource management, and pure tactics. Perhaps the most striking thing is that the area-control part has scoring which often does not particularly favor the player who comes in first; in the mid-late game, it's not unusal for everyone involved to get big payoffs, but for the "winner" to get just a token bonus.

Knizia may not be doing as many true "gamer's games" as he has in the past, with stuff like Amun-Re and Taj Mahal becoming comparatively rare. But still, I think Blue Moon, Blue Moon City, and Beowulf represent some of the best work he's ever done, simple and playable yet with depth, shorter but challenging, well-balanced and perfectly-executed, beautifully produced, with a blend of strategic and tactical skills, and with excellent theme and fun factors. They are perfect blends, providing something for everyone, and to me absolutely represent what good social games should be.

As an aside, I'd agree with Joe Gola that the 3-player version of the game seems slightly tighther. But I've been happy with both 3 and 4.

RATING: N/A
Not one of my favorites. BMC left us feeling cold - like we had all just played solitaire but happened to be using the same set of components to do so. There is no player interaction in this game at all. For example, I tried once to move a couple of the dragons faaar away from my opponents - who simply flew them back to where they wanted on their own turn. I don't see any way to "compete" with your opponents: each player is simply racing to stay ahead of the others with the cards in your hand solely dictating each of your actions rather than a mix of cards in hand and the situation on the tiles in play.

And the beautiful artwork on the tiles are in stark contrast to much of the low-grade kiddie art found on the cards.

The plastic box insert had nothing to do with the components included in this particular game.

Traded for cash.

RATING: 5
Blue Moon City is a move, invest, and then reinvest kind of game where your options from turn to turn are dictated by an 8 suited, 3 ranked deck with art from the Blue Moon Universe. The board is a series of tiles, and each requires certain card types to be tithed so that one can reap some benefits when said building is completed. If dragons are hanging around when you add to a building, then you get some bonus tiles from them, which later transform into crystals, which are the main currency of the game. When each individual tile is finished (which requires between 1 and 4 seperate tithes) then the player who contributed the most is given a special bonus, and everyone who helped at all also gets something. More bonuses are given out when other buildings in the neighbourhood are already complete. As I said earlier, the main economy of the game is crystals, and those are used to complete an obelisk. When a player contributes a target number of tithes to build the obelisk, then the game is over and they win.

If you couldn't tell by the short summary above, this is another one of those Knizia games that has a number of wheels within wheels. Really though, by Dr. K standards, this game isn't that hard to just sit down to an enjoy. Player options on a given turn are kept manageable by one's hand and the amount of space that one can move, yet there is still some leeway for creative play and hand tailoring due to the ability to discard and redraw at the end of each of your turns.

My first impressions of the game were reasonably solid. Lighter medium weight, some randomness inherent because of the cards, but some decent decisions and a pretty fast playtime. I question the amount of player differentiation that can occur though. The obvious strategy thus far seems to be sticking with the pack, because then more tiles are being flipped easily, so you get more bonuses, and more dragons are hanging out and waiting for the par-tay. In this sense, the game seems largely about having that slightly better hand than the other people in the same part of the board, because ultimately where you SHOULD be going seems to be driven by what the other folks are doing. In that sense, I wonder, in retrospect, how much meaningful control I actually have over the game while I'm playing it. It's OK, but after playing it a couple of times I feel that I've seen about all that I need to here.

RATING: N/A
 Blue Moon City is one of the easiest games to pick up, play, and teach. It has a unique and captivating art style that creates the world of Blue Moon. It is constantly gratifying as you rebuild the city - turn over tiles as it is restored to its former glory. The tiles are randomly placed, giving it a new game board every play through. The mechanics constantly reward the players by giving them new cards to make contributions, crystals to make purchases, dragon scales in a race to collect the most. On the surface it's as simple as mixing and matching cards to make combinations, but once you start to take in the special abilities and placement tactics, the game evolves into a highly analytical and thoughtful game. Where do I contribute to get the greatest reward? How can I get other players to do the hard work for me?

A game that brings gamers and non gamers alike. My fiance, and parents love it. A great gateway game into more complex games. A gem that isn't to be missed.

RATING: 9
Components: Stunning artwork on the building tiles and the cards. The iconography is perfectly functional.

Experience: Tense race game. Before playing, I had thought there would be some element of cooperation, with players working together to rebuild the structures; in fact it is a mad dash for crystals by any means available. There isn't enough time to get everything done, to be part of every scoring, to efficiently make offerings, etc., so each move is crucial. There are many opportunities for strategic uses of my cards, which I find to be the core of the game. The cards can be used in so many different combinations it can make my head swirl. Still, I wish there were more strategy in the game; the rapid turnover of cards in my hand, and the quickly changing game board, make it difficult to plan too far ahead.

First Played: 2009-01-05
Last Played: 2011-03-18
Eagerness to Play Again: Soon

Plays (5):
3 players: 5

RATING: 6.5
Move pawn around tiles representing the city, use cards for special benefits (moving dragons, extra move, etc) and spending cards of a given color in order to place a cube on the tile. When cube spots on tile are filled, players with cubes on the tile earn rewards and tile flips. Players periodically move to the center of play area to spend crystals to build the obelisk, which is how the game is won. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

If you are unable to match or beat your opponent to the first few obelisk points at the beginning of the game, there is no way back into the game, unless your opponent gets a disgustingly bad draw of cards. Just plays out like that old game of one hand over your opponents' hand and whoever's hand is at the top of the bat gets first ups...I'm hoping the mechanics change significantly in a 3 to 4 player game.

RATING: 7
(Agost 2012 - 2a mà Amazon.de)
(2-4 jugadors, i funciona especialment bé a 2 jugadors).
Euro 'majories' dissenyat per en Knizia. 
El disseny del joc és molt bonic, amb unes il·lustracions fantàstiques i un tema/història interessant però 'pegat'. El dibuix de les cartes ni el mires, però les llosetes sí les veus i aporten cert relaxament mental.
Les cartes serveixen per 2 coses: o la uses per posar cubitos (majories) o la uses per la seva acció especial. Les que tenen accions especials molt bones, contribueixen poc a les majories, i viceversa. Així que compensa.
És una mena de continuació (almenys pel que fa a la temàtica) d'un joc de miniatures anomenat Blue Moon, en el que varies races (extraterrestres) lluitaven per dominar la ciutat de Blue Moon, però en la lluita la devastaven. En Blue Moon City, s'arriba a una situació de pau, i les diverses races col·laboren per reconstruir la ciutat (ojo!! no és un joc col·laboratiu, i no controlem una raça!, és competitiu, i cada jugador fa ús de totes les races i poders que tenen, en forma de cartes).

RATING: 10
The Blue Moon wars are over, now all the peoples have a group hug and rebuild the city.  Players move their pawns over the city site, playing cards (8 different "suits", one for each of the Blue Moon peoples) to place construction blocks onto face down building tiles.  Once all the slots on a tile are full, the building is complete and anyone participating earns rewards.  Rewards include cards, dragon scales, and the important crystals.  There is a special reward for the best builder, plus nice bonuses from already complete adjacent buildings.  The crystals are used to make offerings to the monument in the centre of the city, and first to make a set number of offerings wins.  The kicker here is each race on the cards has a special ability, loosely themed on the two player card game.  The Flit make you move faster, Khind are weak but useful everywhere, Mimix are better paired up, etc.  Lots to think about - economy of movement, completing buildings to maximise adjacency rewards, how to use the cards to best effect (buildings or abilities).
Recent plays in 2011 have solidified my option - this is a quality design - possibly one of Knizia's best.  Excellent.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Dice Tower Cruise 2017

RATING: 8

I'll admit that I'm a sucker for hand management, but everyone should give this modern classic a chance.

2017-09-16 : First play. I'm sure they're all in there to cover edge cases where the game could become unbalanced, but they remain quirky stuff that you'd expect to be gone after further development of the game. Needs another play

RATING: 10
It's amazing how everything comes together in the game. There's area-control, resource management and a card-driven mechanic, which overall produces a game of remarkably varied gameplay. The game, though simple in rules, can be played "nicely" in a family atmosphere, but also appeals to more cutthroat play among gamers. The depth of strategy and tactics is amazing for such a simple game. Every turn, players make their plans on where to go, plans on what to build, and yet, they have to be prepared to change plans sometimes based on card draws and sometimes based on where they are vis-a-vis other players. If the game was just a straight-out grab for crystals, it might have suffered, but Knizia throws in the dragon scales and that adds a really neat twist into the game. Moreover, the artwork is very beautiful and makes the game pleasing to play. There's no pasted-on theme here. I really do get the sense I'm building the Blue Moon City playing the game. 
Update: What the heck, let's just give this game what it's worth, a 10.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[1 play] Pretty groovy, even though we played slightly wrong--we didn't do the tile layout correctly, though I think it's an official variant.  Fun game with an interesting rhythm.  I was the only one who didn't get a bunch of loot on the first turn, but at a later point I was the only one who had a bunch of cubes out on the board.  You can spend a whole hand of cards having a dramatic extravaganza of a turn, and build back up either through bonuses, or by drawing cards as you spend turns not doing anything but moving.  
	The only thing I disliked about BMC was how loosely attached the theme was.  As so often happens, I was taught the game in a pretty themeless way.  I discovered from skimming the rulebook that there actually was a theme, and considerable backstory about the ruined city, but the theme was just provided, not made integral to the game.  I am even more interested now to play Blue Moon and see how it all ties together.

RATING: 8
aka THE MYSTERIOUS GAME OF MYSTERY(tm). Fun light-middleweight game set in Reiner's Blue Moon universe. It has nothing to do with the mediocre card game (Hi Joe!) Players move their pawns around a board and play cards to build pieces of buildings. When a building (card) is completed, players who contributed to putting it up get rewards. Buildings adjacent to other completed building give even more rewards. Rewards are or ultimately convert into crystals. The crystals are used to build the monolith in the center of the city. Whoever gets a majority of the builds in the monolith wins. The cards have special powers, and comboing those, along with some dragon play (a mechanism that a streamlined cousin of the clunky castle favor mechanism in Caylus) are the ways to win. It won't set the world on fire, but Blue Moon City is a decent pace-changer. Would happily play again.

RATING: 6
Blue Moon City is a pleasant, medium weight building game. There is always a conflict between using your cards for building or for their special powers and some nice spatial restrictions as well. The game moves rather quickly and even speeds up towards the end, and it seems like somewhat of a race where there are always going to be several players within sight of the goal at the end and it's down to technicalities (and maybe a lucky draw or two) who's going to be the winner. It's short enough to not matter that much, though.

Nevertheless, it clearly shows some of Knizia's strengths: fast play with simple rules yet non-trivial decisions.

RATING: 6
I've always wanted to try BMC. I'm a big Knizia fan of course. Overall, there are lots of good ideas here. I like how the cards are used and the way the board works. The artwork is fantastic! For some reason, the game as a whole just doesn't gel together for me. There's future cities, dragon scales, icicle crystals that are offered to an obelisk, etc. Interesting mechanics, but probably one of Knizia's more sloppier pasted on themes. Plus, I think games should be a bit frustrating, but my frustration was constant on one thing: I didn't draw the right cards. This requires more tactical thinking than I think I was planning. Really, you can't have long range plans in BMC because it is almost entirely dependant on what cards you pull. Still, neat decisions here and there make this an interesting game, though certainly not one of my favourites from the good Doctor.

RATING: 6
With a few games under my belt I have to say that Blue Moon city is an exciting little hand and resource management/area control/nonlinear race title.   Blue moon City feels more intuitive than most games. Arguements may be made over strategies, but I find I am never really ever engaged in too deep of thought.  I don't mean to suggest Blue Moon City is ever going to be confused with a strategy game of significant weight, yet for a game in which most decisions are tactical (since the cards  dictate most moves) it sure provides a lot of pure tense fun.  All games are close, and it's always a nail biting dash to the obelisk at game's end. Scales well from 2-4.

Update:  After an initial onslaught of playings this one has been sidelined.  I still like the game for the most, but it feels too mechanical.  It is strange to suggest that a game with card draws being such a prominent mechanic can feel like it needs a randomizer, but that's the way I feel with this one.  BMC is a good opportunity for me to ponder why some like Knizia so much and are vexed by Colovini titles.  For some reason BMC reminds me of a Colovini game without the intereting twists.
Down 7 to 6.

UPDATE: now a six.  After a recent playing my opinion of this has really become ho-hum.  BMC is really an average game at best.

RATING: 5.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Blue Moon City is a good middleweight Euro that offers a pleasant blend of fluid hand management, short-term planning, and collaboration.  The attractive art on the cards and tiles adds significantly to the game's appeal.  It plays quickly, is easy to learn, and scales well from 2 to 4 players (especially good with 3).  It's the brand of overall rock-solid game design that characterizes Knizia's best work.

I like the way that the scope of the game narrows as it progresses.  As more and more of the building projects are completed, you know the game is approaching its end.  Maybe you can see that you'll finish in two turns, but will that be fast enough?

My one gripe about Blue Moon City is that the players late in the turn order are unfairly penalized.  The game ends immediately when someone fulfills the victory condition.  If the first player wins, then she received the benefit of an extra turn.  More often than not, someone else would be able to finish if the game were to continue until the end of the round.  For families, this is not a problem.  Just let the youngest players go first.  But for gamers it's a big problem, and I think it needs to be addressed.  It wouldn't be hard.  Just play until the end of the round, with no limit to the number of slots on the monument.  Break ties according to the number of remaining crystals.  Early players would still have the first crack at the cheapest monument slots, but the big issue is the sudden death ending.

Knizia's original design for Blue Moon City called for all dragon scales to be discarded after each scoring - even the players who didn't get any points.  I definitely like this rule better than Kosmos' more forgiving method, and suggest you try it.

RATING: 7.5
A game that is interesting yet fast paced and tight. 

The game has the curious element that you are working to get resources and you are then using those resources to generate points. The two step process makes the game just a little more about process than many resource allocation games.

It restores my faith in the church of Knizia.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Fifteen plays. A fun tactical middleweight(ish) game based on the kick-ass card game. What makes the game interesting is that most of the cards are dual-purpose, and so players can use them to build or turn them in for some other advantage (increased movement, movement of dragons, et cetera). There's also some strategy involved in the decisions regarding the areas on which to focus, the pacing of one's expenditure of cards, whether one wants to spend a few cards and share profits or spend a lot and shut others out, et cetera. Another interesting facet is that the mechanisms of the game encourage players to stay in the vicinity of their opponents and react to their choices, so there's never a feeling of multiplayer solitaire. In the end, it doesn't quite have the depth of the best Knizia games, but it makes up for this by presenting the players with delightful (and sometimes agonizing) puzzles on each turn. One caveat: I preferred the three-player game to the four-player game, as the playing time of the three-player game was more in keeping with the weight, and I also felt like I was more connected to the other players. The two-player game works just fine.

There is an expansion with four additional tiles. It's not really necessary, but it doesn't hurt.

RATING: 9
A fun game of maximizing your actions.  You are juggling almost all aspects (cards, tiles, cubes, dragons, crystals) to squeeze the best out of each turn.  Cards have dual uses and choices are difficult on how and when to use each card, with hand size being an issue.  Do you go in for some of many tiles, or try to get all the reward on just a few?  Contribute for victory now when it is cheaper, or take another opportunity to get resources, and maybe have victory contributions become expensive?  Go for that tile on the side and risk being too far to do anything good your next turn, or do something closer?
A favorite of my wife and son, it is often requested.  We've also taught it to my brother and his wife with good reception.

RATING: 5
[Initial Impression: 5]  I'm something of a Knizia-holic, but I wasn't particularly smitten with this area influence/resource management/race game.  The most interesting aspect of it is that many cards have two potential uses: for the special powers, or for the face value for offerings.  A second interesting bit is that early planning for minority bonuses can pay off later in the game.  The strategy seems to be to go around seeding the board early with your offerings, so that you get at least some crystals when most city tiles score, choose a few high-value tiles to score highly in, and make sure to have at least three dragon scales for each of those scorings. Late in the game, a person can get a lot of crystals on other player's turns and then just head to the city center to build the obelisk.  (That worked for me, anyway.) I didn't like that once a lot of regions had scored, some cards suddenly became quite valueless, which virtually kills the hand management aspect.  Overall, the game feels like playing a shell game with the designer - what's important is building in the obelisk, but I'm going to distract you with these dragon scales and crystals and card effects.  If you can follow along, remember what's important, and exploit the trade-off ratios, you'll do well at the shell game. It will feel like a close race, as several people near the end goal simultaneously, but it ultimately seems like that relative closeness is contrived.  Interesting implementation, but the game doesn't have enough depth or fun to make me anticipate a return visit to Blue Moon City.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Blue Moon City is a game where card accumulation allows acquisition of crystals, which in turn allows offerings at the Obelisk- the only points that matter in the end.

The trick is being in the right place(s) at the right time(s), with the right bonuses in play.

Basically the theme is rebuilding the eponymous City, where different buildings require different race resources - the cards.  Depending on the building and the bonuses from dragons and other completed buildings, the rewards for being part of the effort to rebuild can be small, or quite large.

Players can completely restore a building, or can contribute some of the resources and share the rewards with the other players assisting- of course those who do the most, get the most in the form of an addtional bonus.

Accumulated crystals allow offerings to the obelisk- and the player who makes the most or who donates a scaled number of times first wins.

I rated the game a 6.75 on 1st play (based on the printed ruleset), but after restoring the Doktor's risk/reward rules re: rewards for Dragon Scales I have increased it to a 7.

RATING: 8
Q:  What do you get if Nanking, Dresden and Hiroshima have an absinthe-fueled ménage à trois—and you and your friends were left to clean up the mess?

A:  [i]Blue Moon City[/i]!
  
As is usual in Knizia games, you typically want to do just one more thing per turn than you're allowed, leading to the agony of coming up one move short (or not) for the win.  The overall strategic feel is more parasitic than cooperative—figuring out how best to piggy-back on other people's hard work.

All our games to date have been very close... this sort of "many-stooges-through-one-doorway-simultaneously" finish makes for a tense (but fun) game.

RATING: 9
Blue Moon City is a pretty clever little game for four that actually rewards cooperative play. It seems unusually simple at first but there's some real depth in clever cardplay hidden away not to mention resource optimization. The way the cards work for the races reminds me a lot of Blue Moon as well which adds a little bit to the experience for fans of the original Knizia masterpiece.  I'm not too sure if BMC got robbed at Spiel des Jahres by Thurn and Taxis, but I do know that I probably wouldn't pass up a play of this game over most others.  The race for the obelisk every few turns is simply too priceless.

Update:  Bumped my rating up after a two player game and decided that BMC was robbed at SdJ.  This game is very good as a two player title, way more than I would have ever anticipated.  All of the mechanics feel tighter and there's a better sense of clever cardplay and tension.  It's OK for four, good with three, but downright beautiful with two.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
I finally got a chance to play this one.  I was excited when it first came out, but then got disillusioned when I read the rules.  When I finally played it, it's not that bad at all.  It really is a neat hand management game that gives you many different ways to spend your cards.

Still, in the end, it just didn't do anything novel.  It was cute and enjoyable, but it didn't grab me.

I will say, it had *great* iconography.

Feb09:

Over time, I'd say all of the cards are equally useful.  There's not suit that I cringe about drawing.

Some minor points:

I have made the expansion tiles from the free downloads.  They're worth it, although the one that lets you draw extra cards might need neutered down to 1 extra card.  The others are fun and different.

I also enjoy Knizia's original rules about those with less than 3 scales during a reckoning getting nothing [b]and[/b] losing their current scales.


I almost [b]always[/b] discard 2 cards at the end of every turn.  I usually dig for some "3" value cards to make efficient building actions.  Other than that, I just like always sculpting my hand into what I want or need for the current moment.

There is an interesting cooperative aspect.  With multiple players it becomes an odd dilemma of deciding who to assist and who to mooch on.  There's not too many other games like that.

And above all else, my wife just loves that game.  It's very pretty and she likes the positive vibe that comes with building up the city.

There is also a pretty heavy skill quotient.  I've gotten noticeably better over time at efficiently using my cards.  I can constantly keep a 6+ card hand and still complete buildings every 2-3 turns.  It really keeps your options open.

RATING: 7.5
Ideal Players: 4

Description: Collect cards to build buildings, which give you crystals used to buy victory points. Get bonus points by summoning dragons or being the one to complete a building.

Pro: Complex enough to allow for interesting strategy and choices. No direct conflict. The theme and mechanics fit well together.

Con: Very few people are familiar with the theme. It's easily too complicated for some players. Random card draws can make or break a player.

RATING: 5
2014-09-15

Initial Rating: 5.5 (September 2014)

Ok game. I still haven’t played Blue Moon, but I really like games that continued the thematic setting of another game (still Blue Moon City is quite theme less as a good Knizia should be). I really liked the game components and the game art is ok. 

The game is very simple and you can teach it under 8 minutes. I like the area control aspect of the game. The game plays quick. Otherwise I would rate it a lot lower. It looks gorgeous on the table.

I feel Blue Moon City has lots of obvious decisions with your current hand, so it is pure tactics and I feel luck decides who wins most of the time. The most interesting decision is how you use your cards (as a number or as a power), perhaps is less random with 2 players only. Still I had a fun time playing it and I would play again if requested but it is way too light for my tastes and the luck of the draw seems overpowering. My GF like the game so perhaps I will buy it.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Players move their marker up to two spaces at a time around a grid of "destroyed buildings". Play cards to contribute to the restoration of those buildings. The same cards played differently have special powers. Highest contributor to each building gets the most reward.

The game is a combination of hand management through card drafting, and distributing your contributions most efficiently on the board.

Played about a dozen games now with just my wife. I can see where the strategy and dynamics would be significantly as a 3P game too. In any case there's a bit of Carc H&G in this (limited cubes, the ones on the Obelisk score the big points but you can't get 'em back) along with the "area majority" and excellent hand management possibilities.

So you get some leverage by manipulating the dragons well. And most of your leverage by making sure you get a cube in as many of the buildings being closed as you can.

With 2P it's about trying to finish some [i]valuable[/i] buildings solo, and making sure the other player can't. Also it pays to be cautious with your cards... there's plenty of real estate to work on and better to do it with efficient card combinations. (That's where timing visiting the obelisk comes in ... a chance to improve your hand while still doing something useful.)

With 3P or more I expect it'd be much more about getting in on more buildings (rather than trying to dominate them) and finishing buildings in a sequence to make the neighbour bonuses more favourable to you. Which would more often mean jumping in with less efficient card combos.

RATING: 6
I can see the fascination with this game... special powers for some of the cards, random set-up, a Knizia design... but, after playing, it seems to almost play itself.  My only win came about because the two players before me didn't claim an area that I needed for victory.

Every player has an equal chance to win, which is very typical of Knizia designs, but because of the card driven element, a run of bad luck can be devastating.  A decent SdJ nominee... but only a '6' rating from me.

RATING: 8
Very good boardgame and quite different from the two-player cardgame, although the beautiful art work and intriguing fantasy world have been kept. Blue Moon City is Knizia's card-driven wargame with a nice twist: cards are used either for their "event" or their "ops value", but contrary to CDW the theme/story is rebuilding the destructed city and not the simulation of some war. Hand management combined with set-collection - use the dragons to your advantage! **** Plays very well with three or four players.

RATING: 5
After a few plays I'm still not sure what I think of this game. It's nicely packaged and as is true with the card game, it's GORGEOUS to look at, but I'm not sure I get the big deal. You move, play cards that match the color you're on and get crystals (points). Then you turn in the points for end game credit. Here's the problem I have... it's all based on card draw but almost every move you have a way of getting a card match somehow. Every person is basically pulling in the same amount of crystals. In a four player game everyone comes one turn away from winning. It just seems like an excercise rather than a game. Also, the artwork as previously stated is Gorgeous but basically has absolutely NOTHING TO DO WITH THE CARDS OR THE GAME. See the numbers in the upper part of the cards? That bar of number and symbol is all you need. So basically you could play this game with pennies and a poker deck. I always feel a little cheated when I'm asked to pay $50 for something that can be compared to a game I made up as a kid using household items. There's got to be something better out there to play than this.

RATING: N/A
Hard to Find (Even online - get from BBG) - reprint coming!!

Blue Moon City - the board game - picks up where the two-player game, (Blue Moon), ended: the reconstruction of the destroyed city of Blue Moon. The board, illustrated by Franz Vohwinkel as well as many well-known American fantasy artists, consists of 21 large building tiles, which show building plans on one side and the buildings in their reconstructed glory on the other. As in the 2-player game, the game includes 3 large molded plastic dragons.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[2006.Dec.14] Another Reiner Knizia game that is surely destined to become a well-respected classic. This time he has provided us with transformation-style game with a twist&mdash;the game is a pseudo-cooperative. Players must carefully convert the cards in their hands into intermediary resources (these are "crystals" in the game, represented by thick cardboard disks). Proper hand management, board positioning, as well as flexibility are key during this step. These intermediary resources are in turn used to achieve the end-game goal. Naturally, the good Doctor provides us with more than one avenue to achieve this goal. With fast-paced turns, this game easily keeps players involved, and interested, in the game. Recycling the superb art depicted in the Blue Moon card game, the Fantasy Flight Games edition is a work of art in itself and is a pleasure to play. But don't be fooled by the title, because although the game is called Blue Moon City&mdash;other than this theme, and what a theme it is!&mdash;the game is very different from the Blue Moon card game. May the dragons watch you while you rebuild the City!

[2007.Jan.01] The addition of the four (4) expansion tiles (available from Spielbox Magazin; http://www.spielbox-magazin.de/smspecials.php) simply enhances the flavor, playability, and strategic "board play" of this game.

:star::star::star::star::star:

RATING: 6
LIKES:

* Interesting conflict of using cards for their special action versus playing sets to claim various spots on the different locations to earn crystals which allow you to earn points.
* There isn't a necessity to win first place on the different squares.  Frequently, there can be great value just to be present, particularly in the mid to late game as other awards are available from completed tiles!
* Board changes each game as the tiles change from game to game - although each tile is always played.

DISLIKES:

* It doesn't bother me so much, but some people think there might be too much potential in luck of the draw that can mess over someone over the course of the game.  
* It does not play well with 2 in my perspective.  Play only with 3 or 4.  I will never again play with only two.

RATING: 7.5
This is a very interesting game with an interesting and unique mechanic that drives the construction of buildings for rewards of various types.  Choosing which building to make your offering is often difficult as it often forces you to weigh the benefits of several competing possibilities.  Other tough decisions include whether to use a card for offerings or for its power, whether to discard at the end of a turn and whether to run to the obelisk to make an offering or continue making offerings at builings and gathering more crystals.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The first few games of this felt a little anticlimactic.  One of the problems is that this game is just [i]too[/i] balanced.  In every game we've played, the loser would have been able to win the next turn.  While some might find this photo-finish ending a feature, it made the game feel pretty arbitrary to us.  Part of the problem is that the bonuses for actually completing the buildings with the most cubes are pretty small.  You just need to contribute just one cube to each building in order to reap most of the benefits when they get completed.  As a result, the two player game feels a lot like [GAMEID=17106], as you walk around contributing to buildings until finally someone has to complete one to get their cubes back.

Don't get me wrong, I think that there is a good game here.  Unfortunately, the problem is that the game feels like a light game, but is deceptively deep, especially for just two players.  Though it seems like the cardplay should be the major focus of the game, it's a much more tactical and positional game.  Since the advantages each turn are so small, in order to play well requires a lot of thought to optimize each turn.  It's the smallest efficiencies that can win you the game.

This is probably better with more people than with just two, when players can form shifting alliances to cooperate to finish a building to gain a clearer advantage over the others.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
UPDATE: BMC is a fine game but after more plays feels the same. I feel its an excellent introductory game as it is very easy to learn.

Have played several games with 4 and one with 2. All are close affairs - have not seen anyone win by more than one cube. In 2 player game one must watch that you do not scatter your cubes - used both in marking your contributions to rebuilding various parts of BMC and for marking for victory. Since you have 10 cubes and need 6 to win in 2 player more attention is needed on this aspect.

Best of all, the people that I can game with the most often seem to like the game a lot. Rules are easy and play time runs maybe an hour or so.

Fundamentally a hand maangement game that plays quickly and has considerable balance.

Simple but rich and very well streamlined mechanics marred by an unfortunately high luck factor. Short downtimes, fast session times, involving and strategic but not brain-burning, thematic but game play and mechanics over theme; there are few things to complain about and much to praise. Recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Review after my first play: I'm not sure what to say about this game - it's an area influence game combined with a hand-management-focused cardgame; I found it hard to strategize during the game, partially because there were so many colors of cards and so many options for how to use them (and it was so difficult to get sets of similarly-colored cards).  Unlike most Knizia games, I didn't see a clear set of paths that all could lead to victory - instead it felt like I was wandering through a forest stumbling along trying to get crystals and scales without a clear idea of what was the best way to do it.  Needless to say, I lost.  I clearly need to play this more, but right now I wonder if the hype is based more on the fact that the artwork is from Blue Moon or that the gameplay is actually that great.  

After the second play: While I understand more about the game (and the hand management and functions of the cards make more sense), I still seemed to be a few turns behind my competitors.  This definitely seems like a game where a couple of people can team up and help each other, which is probably not ideal.  I definitely enjoyed it more, though, and want to try it again.

After the third play: I'm starting to get the hang of this more; choosing which tiles to flip is clearly key, and is nicely tactical (as what everyone else is doing necessarily dictates where you go, as already-flipped tiles give you bonuses).  I've also increased my ranking from 7 to 7.5.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
My wife really likes this game so that alone makes it worth owning.  The player interaction is low so my son didn't care much for it.  Even though I like many multi-player solitaire type games, this one wasn't quite as interesting as many others.  I tend to prefer games in which decisions made in earlier turns affect your long term strategy.  In other words, Blue Moon City is more about short term tactics and less about long term strategy.  Being able to analyze the current board position and make the most of whatever cards you have is the name of the game.  The good news is that it plays fast and usually results in close finishes.

RATING: 7

Un jeu amusant, facile et rapide de Rainer Knizia. Un genre de mixte entre un jeu de carte et un "city builder". Les mécaniques sont originales, le jeu est facile à enseigner et agréable à jouer. Ce n'est pas le meilleur jeu du docteur Knizia mais c'est un très bon jeu.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
After just one play (plus a solo game to learn the rules)... brilliant game. A great game where there is a lot to think about. Cards are very well balanced and you are constantly faced with interesting choices of where to build and what cards to use, which to hold onto for future turns and which to discard in hopes of getting better ones. The game is both thematic and yet mathematically elegant. One nice feature I like is that the game doesn't feel as "mathy" and dry as some Euros can sometimes feel (especially from designers like Knizia). This is a good solid family style game but yet definitely has appeal to the gamer type as well. I feel the game has a pretty wide appeal as it's solidly in the middleweight category and another plus is the rules are very simple to learn and teach. The only part you really need to cue in on is the various card powers which takes a bit of explaining but if you keep a player aid or 2 handy for the players the first time they play... these will be learned very quickly. There are just enough card powers to make the game interesting and diverse without swamping players and making things overly complicated and slowing pace to a crawl. A balance that many other games fail to achieve.

The first game or two, players will certainly have to think about their actions and best moves so there will be some downtime but that will go down as players learn what cards to use and what actions to take at different stages of the game.

All in all, one of the better middle weight games in my collection and I can certainly see my rating possibly rising to a 9 with more plays. I can't see a rating drop below 8 at this point as the game play is the type that appeals to me... quick, yet interesting with multiple considerations to think about every turn.

RATING: 7.5
In this Knizia game, there are 21 tiles. Each tile has one or more numbers with the color of cards that can be played on that tile.  If you play the right number and color of cards, you can claim one of those numbers by placing your cube there. Once the numbers are all full, the tile is scored like an area control mechanic: the first place player with the most cubes gets a certain reward, but all players will get a reward just for being present. The rewards are getting more cards, crystals or dragon skin. Crystals allow you to put cubes in the obelisk, where you need 4 cubes to win. The cards represent the races of Ble Moon (eg Flit, Aqua, etc) and some cards give special abilities eg the '1' Flit card allows you to move your token anywhere on the board, the 1' Aqua card allows you to move the blue dragon, etc. The number of dragon skin you collect allow you to earn more crystals. This is a good fast paced game that easily plays in under an hour, with the tension increased as each opponent gets closer to the victory conditions.

RATING: 7
Beautiful game, easy to teach and very entertaining.  With the random placement of the buildings, plays will differentiate from each other.  Even non-gamers can enjoy it.  Absolutely recommend and what do you know, it shines with two-players.

My only concern is that the game has been specifically designed to be a close-race to the very end.  Every game I played ended with the winner just one step ahead of another player.  It felt a little bit artificial if that makes any sense.

Update:  I heard that the "artificial" close race was made to make the game more casual and friendly.

Update 2:  The OOP status of this game led me to find another game with a similar mechanism that might be overall better:  Istanbul.

RATING: 6.7
It's a hand management tactical card game with reflections on a random tile-made map that plays out very fast.

The theme is paper thin, as with most Knizia's, but this one has high quality components and superb illustrations that make up a bit for it.

It's a light gateway-like game, but still, your hand of cards allows you for some options and some minor medium term planification (that will go awry half of the times, especially with 4 players).

It has attractive mechanics that mitigate the luck factor and intense interaction (no direct attacks, but you compete for the board spots and at the same time, you need your rivals to fill your tiles; you'll lose if you go your on way).

First impression [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3912520#item3912520]here[/url].

RATING: 6
A fun little tile flipping game. I'm not in love with it, but it's one I'll play quite a few times without complaining.

Verdict:

What I Like: Easy to teach, fairly quick to play. Great card artwork, and the fact that no two cards are the same is fantastic.

What I Don't Like: It can feel too simple. The last few turns are spent hoping you get the card draws you need to win it and/or your opponents do not. Therefore a win rarely feels entirely earned. Though well drawn, the tiles color pallet is bland and uninteresting.

RATING: 6
[b]Blue Moon City[/b] was sold via auction 8/8/15.
Received in a trade 10/31/14

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]This one started out strong and ended up fizzling out after more plays. What really soured me on the game was the lack of variety in how the game played out. Even though the layout of buildings changed from game to game it really didn't change much. It basically just meant you were looking for different colored cards to play. The 2p experience was okay, but there was a lot less conflict over who was going to build each building and the game devolved into a race with a predictable outcome of who would score the last cube in the tower. 

It sounds like I really don't like the game, which isn't true, I'm just not interested in keeping games in our collection that will only get played once or twice a year and that's about how often I'd want to play BMC. However; if someone else pulled out a copy I'd happily play.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- I love the artwork on the tiles (the buildings), most of the characters (there are a few that I really don't care for) and the dragon pieces are great.
- Plays well with 2, although it does become even more of a race where you may try to build each site on your own.
- An interesting twist on area control, since you can build where someone else has you may be able to share in some of the benefits for building that site.
- Dragons: the addition of the dragons/scales adds another layer of depth to the game because you can get quite a few crystals if you have the majority of scales.
- Cube management: my son and I played a 2p game and I almost lost because I did not have a cube left to build in the temple.
- Hand management and all the different card powers/combos.
- Random tile layout: This really changes how you play the game since getting adjacent building bonuses can be very important.

This is an excellent game that really deserves a reprint, I really haven't played anything quite like it.[/i]

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*起始版塊放中間，宮殿跟三神廟版塊圍繞，其餘隨機擺，鱗片按人數使用(2:9,3:12,4:15)，玩家代表物放在起始版塊，每人八張牌
*玩家行動依序執行:移動1~2格(不能不動)>在建築物用卡片貢獻，在起始版塊可貢獻水晶塔>棄0~2張卡，並抽棄牌數+2張卡，牌庫不夠用將棄牌洗進去
*建築是看你出的牌顏色大於等於建築物的該格數字就可以放標記物，最後一個放完就轉過來，並領取左上角獎賞，建設最多的(平手最左的)全拿，其它的拿星號下面的，全部人也會得鄰近完成版塊的獎勵，如果有龍在上面，幾隻就或得幾個鱗片，獲得的卡片下輪才能用
*鱗片拿完就記水晶(如果當下不夠拿就用記的，數字還是算)，最多的六個，兩人平手各三個，超過三個的三個，有拿到水晶的就還鱗片
*起始版塊就是用水晶建設，勝利條件為看誰在水晶塔建立最多(2:6,3:5,4:3)
*個人標記數有限，如果全部都用完還沒達到貢獻數，算平手
*變體，除起始版塊外，其它都隨機放置

RATING: 7
A neat race game. Multiple card powers create interesting decisions each turn - do I use them to get cubes on the board (which will eventually give me the crystals I need to score a VP) or do I get more benefit from using its special power somewhere else. The special powers can also provide ways to get onto cube spaces (which may be about to score) that you may not otherwise have been able to because you lacked that colour. So theres's a nice decision to make each turn on how to blow your cards ... or conserve them for later wiser use and simply draw more for now. The trick to the cube building is to offer enticements so that other people will help you out (because they only score when completed), and that scratch my back, scratch your back feel is nicely different from other games. An enjoyable card game with oomph.

What I don't like:

RATING: 7
Had a lot of fun playing this one. The dragons are cute, and add an interesting element of strategy to an otherwise routine route optimization and hand management game. The art on the cards is good, but weird, and fits the background of the game world. Each of the factions has its own distinct feel, making them a welcome change from the Borg like sameness we see in other games. I also like the semi-random setup of the board, as it lends itself well to repeated plays. 

When I first saw this game, my initial thought was "Thank god; no elves, dwarves or hobbits." I'm so sick of seeing fantasy worlds based on Tolkien's writings. No offense, but I like J.R.R. best when he stays confined to his books. The man's dead, so let's leave him alone already. 

When someone asked me to describe Blue Moon City to them.  I said: "It's a race game, where you scurry around rebuilding a city, and you use cards as the building material. The dragons like to watch you do stuff, and reward you if you please them. Sounds a bit kinky, but its a lot of fun." 

Blue moon city IS a bit kinky, but in a good way. At its best, it tickles your brain, and satisfies that urge for non-confrontational competition. The weird theme and art may throw some people off, but the game is well worth learning to play.

RATING: 6
Mentally, I enthusiastic with the design as it has many elements I look for: the deck distribution is uniform so easy to remember, winning condition is "first to" (placing a set # of cubes) which is visually monitored; each game is randomly laid out so this and cards picked keeps the game play different and refreshing. For such a relatively simple gameplay there are subtleties to explore: hand card management (speculating and discarding), cube management and the subgame race for dragon scales as an alternate route to pick up crystals. Update: I don't know, I think emotionally I'm starting to go cold on Blue Moon City. The fantasy theme is offputting, explaining the game and the card effects is offputting but the cooperative involvement in building can be more offputting as there can be kingmaking situations.

RATING: 8
Pros:
 * Setup is pretty quick and the game plays quickly with little AP.
 * The dragon-scales "mini game" is fun and reminds us of the 1-through-6 "mini-game" in Finca.
 * The powers of the cards are simple to understand but also able to be used in clever combinations, giving you the ability to mitigate the luck of the draw.
 * Giving the lower numbered cards the better powers makes for some difficult decisions.
 * We're particularly fond of games that have an "achieve X and you win" goal; you could have a huge stash of crystals and still lose this game.
 * The game is well balanced and an early lead doesn't result in a runaway leader.

Cons:
 * Four of the tiles are supposed to be placed adjacent to the obelisk in the center, but they aren't marked. If you keep them together when you put the game away, it's not an issue.
 * The game is a bit "fiddly" and you'll find yourself needing to be a bit careful when calculating and handing out rewards, flipping tiles and putting pieces back. It's not a problem, but you may want to assign a "banker" who pays attention to details.

= 2017-12-11 - R: 8

Played again for the first time in almost 5 years. This game has aged very well and is making me question why I bother keeping so many more recent games.

One of the first designer games I purchased and still one of the best. Bumping the rating.

(moved session comments into play log)

= 2013-01-15 - R: 7.5

Purchased this game way back in April, 2012 and we finally got around to playing it two days ago.

Many reviews for this game mentioned that it often came down to a single turn or a couple crystals. Our first game certainly lived up to this.

We played our second game last night. As a strong indicator of how good this game is, we found ourselves thinking about it quite a bit over the last two days. I'm curious what the long term replayability of the game will be.

Starting this one at 7.5.

RATING: 7.5
I tried out the card game (and was unimpressed, mainly by the excessive reading and familiarizing with vastly different card abilities that is required, and more importantly at the unbalancedness of the decks (as proven by the AI program test results)), and read a lot about the board game, and didn't have many expectations. Boy, were the boardgame reviews and comments I read misleading! This is a completely different game than the card game - it's actually BALANCED! There are a limited number of abilities to learn (easily digestable in one focused play), and it's not basically a game of War! But it keeps the only strengths of the cardgame - the theme, characters, and art. Very pleasant surprise.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow.  What a great game!

We've now played this one a couple of game nights in a row, and each game gets better.  Next time out we are going to put in the expansion tiles.

I think the best thing about this game so far is that it is SO well balanced.  Every game has come down to the last play with multiple players on the cusp of winning, and there was only one game so far that had anyone trailing by a significant amount.

There is a lot of cooperation, yet a lot of competition at the same time.  The options and tradeoffs are interesting (and critical) on every turn, but even our group's most paralysis-prone players seem to be able to make decisions quickly.

What a game.  The only reasons it's not a 10 yet is that I have not played it enough, but this one may pass Samurai into my #1 slot soon...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The major flaw in this game is that after the first couple of turns when you spend your cards, all of your options are strictly controlled by what cards you happened to draw the previous turn, resulting in all strategies being frustrated or allowed by luck alone.  Result of the one four-player game I played: the winner built 4 cubes, to 1-1-0.  I think I would raise the rating by two or three points if every player started with a full deck of cards and could spend up to 6 of them per turn in any order they chose, with no replacements, thus eliminating the collosal impact that luck has on the game.  But the game doesn't include that many cards.  Bah, as the game stands.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I don't know anything about the Blue Moon world. In this game, players manage their hand of cards in order to complete buildings. Scoring each building gives reward to each player who contributed and gives better reward to the "master builder". Each completed building gives a bonus to orthogonally adjacent buildings, when they are scored. Each suit of cards has also a special power (moving dragon, better speed for your pawn...). The most important reward for scoring buildings are crystals. You can spend crystals to make offers to the obelisk. The game ends as soon as one player has make a certain number of offers to the obelisk, depending on the number of players (2 players: 6 offers, 3 players: 5 offers, 4 players: 4 offers). That player is the winner.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Don't compare this game to Blue Moon, they have almost nothing in common. The board is assembled mostly randomly with areas that provide a number of dragon scales, cards, and/or crystals as rewards. Areas contain 1-4 boxes, each box contains a colored number, and a player must play cards in the right color adding up to that number in order to play a control cube there. Once all boxes are filled, the land is conquered, everyone gets a reward, and the majority holder gets a bonus. If the area is next to any adjacent areas that have previously been conquered, they also confer a payment. You have to move to an area to play cards there, and there's a range limit, so between that and adjacent bonuses, there's a significant positional element. Scales are essentially traded off for crystals. Crystals are used to purchase real estate on the "oblisk" in the center of the board. Buy four spots on the oblisk to win the game. I liked it. The three dragons are back from Blue Moon, but they're not used for scoring. Rather they provide a scale bonus if you manage to summon them to an area just before you conquer it. Several factors to consider, but nothing too heavy on the brain.

RATING: 8.7
[COLOR=#663300][b][size=11]Blue Moon City[/size][/b][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#663300][i]Note: My numeric ratings disregard BGG's associated verbal descriptions.[/i][/COLOR]

[COLOR=#006600]Version played: [/COLOR]2006 Fantasy Flight, English
[COLOR=#006600]Play History: [/COLOR]
 [COLOR=#006600] • 3 players: [/COLOR] Several times
 [COLOR=#006600] • 4 players: [/COLOR] Several times

[COLOR=#006600]Instructions: [/COLOR][COLOR=#CC0000]Need to evaluate.[/COLOR]
[COLOR=#006600]Learning Curve: [/COLOR]Quick
[COLOR=#006600]Depth: [/COLOR]Fair to middling.
[COLOR=#006600]Luck: [/COLOR]Low.  Yes, card draws are random, but there' usually something that can be done with any hand.
[COLOR=#006600]Downtime: [/COLOR]Turns move relatively quickly.
[COLOR=#006600]Fiddliness: [/COLOR]While there are lots of bits to be placed, and placards to be flipped, it doesn't feel fiddly to me.
[COLOR=#006600]Components: [/COLOR]Top notch.  Beautiful artwork on cards and placards.  Hefty dragon figurines.
[COLOR=#006600]Player Interaction: [/COLOR]More than there first appears.  Placing cubes thoughtfully can entice or defer opponents' interest.
[COLOR=#006600]Conflict: [/COLOR]None to speak of.
[COLOR=#006600]Theme: [/COLOR]The ostensible theme is thin, but the atmosphere is rich due to the artwork.

[COLOR=#006600]Travel related: [/COLOR][COLOR=#CC0000]Unsuitable.[/COLOR]
 [COLOR=#006600] • Size: [/COLOR]Large
 [COLOR=#006600] • Weight: [/COLOR]Heavy
 [COLOR=#006600] • Table Required: [/COLOR]Yes
 [COLOR=#006600] • Footprint: [/COLOR]Large (and without a custom board, unwieldy)
 [COLOR=#006600] • Component Trackability: [/COLOR]Poor
 [COLOR=#006600] • Replaceability: [/COLOR]Available as of Jan 2010

[COLOR=#006600][b]Comments: [/b][/COLOR]

Addictive.

Neighborhood bonuses are perhaps the most interesting element, as the order in which they are revealed can entirely change the relative value of surrounding tiles.  This can be great when the revealed bonus is highly valuable and you have a presence on the surrounding tiles.  But if you don't have such a presence, swooping in and revealing a low value bonus [b]before[/b] a neighboring high value bonus is revealed can prevent your opponents from cleaning up.

This game is joining my favorites.  I find that I always enjoy it.  Some games warrant great ratings based on various attributes, but the actual [i]play[/i] may feel like work to me.  Not so with Blue Moon City.  Based on attributes such as its variable board, production quality, ease of play, and such, I think Blue Moon City does warrant relatively high ratings, but my own experience of the game goes beyond objective analysis -- I just find it really fun.  Hence, I've continued to revise my rating a bit higher after every play.

[COLOR=#006600][b]Why we own it: [/b][/COLOR] 

If I have to name a deciding feature, it's the neighborhood bonuses, which remain once a tile is completed and flipped.  But of course the factor really responsible for our decision to give this game space in our crowded house is that we have fun playing it.

RATING: 9
Should have won the SdJ 2006. This is Kniza at his middleweight best. The Blue Moon wars are over and players use a hand of cards to contribute towards the rebuilding of the city. These cards have other special abilities (wild, change the suit of another card, attracts a dragon, etc). Each turn, a player moves his piece and plays cards to build. There are crystals given to players for building and for collecting dragon scales. These crystals are used to put a temple stone on the Obelisk. The first player to play a set number of obelisk stones wins!

The tension in this game is agonising. Players contantly trying to jostle for position and timing of their building; often hoping their opponents finish off the buildings to get THEM a bonus. The elements of dragon scale scoring and card combinations are clever and it's very much a race game of sorts. The dragons and great artwork is rehashed from the card game and I wished they had kept the Tarot sized cards. The board placement can get fiddly and messy but these are just little flaws in the scheme of things. Faidutti has rightly compared this to Cleopatra (DoW) as both are building and hand management games. Blue Moon City has a slight edge in terms of offering better meshed mechanics and a shorter, tighter and tenser experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
It has been years since I've been excited about a Knizia boardgame.  I really wanted to like this and lo and behold, I actually do.  There is a wonderful mix between hand management, movement and timing.  I love how you want to get a piece of other players actions so that they can do the work for you.  There is a great sense of "Keeping up with the Joneses" as you clearly want to go out and earn more but you simply must come into the center to earn your victory, the longer you put it off the more of a pain in the ass it is going to be.

It seems that three players would work the best for this as 4 may get too chaotic as too much can happen (along with the time spent) between your turns.  Great forward planning, need for flexibility, and reactionary tactics keep this game lively until the end.  Way to get back into the game Knizia.

RATING: 8
