=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The board consists of a central tile square surrounded by tiles that are placed in a particular configuration (square with projections on each side) but with each tile randomly chosen. Tokens start in the middle. The object is to build 4 stones of the Obelisk ... the first 4 built each cost 7 crystals, then costs escalate all the way up to 12. The first player to build all 4 wins. You get crystals by moving around the board and interacting with the tiles. The tiles each require various combinations of cards (you draw 2+n each turn, where n is a number of cards you discard from your hand, from 0-2) played as sets, to complete 1-3 goals. When all goals on a tile are completed, everyone who contributed gets a reward. In addition, the person completing the majority of goals gets a bonus. The rewards are generally crystals, but can also include additional cards and dragon scales. Cards can be used towards these goals, but also for their special powers (different for each of the 5 races/colors). For example, you can discard cards to call the 3 dragons into play or move them onto your tile. If you complete a goal, each dragon present on your tile rewards you with a dragon scale. When all the dragon scales have been awarded, players earn crystals depending on how many scales they have (most are then returned to stock). The interesting thing about the board is that after all goals of a tile are complete, it is turned upside down. From then on, it adds a bonus reward to all adjacent tiles.

The game was just OK. You move around, gather resources, get to the central tile and try to buy the 4 spots as cheaply as possibly. You have to be efficient, and it's interesting how completing 1 tile's goals might set up the other players who are working on adjacent tiles. I like the tile-flipping mechanism where 1 tile adds to the rewards of several others, but there just didn't seem to be very much [i]game[/i] or [i]fun[/i] here!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like the resource management aspect of this.  It feels cooperative rather than competitive much of the time.

RATING: 7.5
I quite enjoyed this game, despite not so much enjoying the original Blue Moon 2-player card game.  I tend to find games that are strictly card-based to be personally uninteresting and I typically need a game with an evolving board situation in order to maintain my interest.  Here, the good doctor has combined the hand management of a card game with the movement and flow of making deliveries.  It's a dynamic, fast, and exciting race (though it would probably be much less so with only 2 players - I'd stick to 3 or 4 for this one).  The physical components are quite nice, though I'm quite bitter that my pawn is terribly boring compared to those cool dragons!  The card art is pretty well done, though kinda weird.  Once you get over the hurdle of learning the powers of all the different card colours, the game is easy to dive into and understand (I gave it a "game weight" rating of 2, though in my head I was thinking it was closer to 2.35, and was quite amused to discover that BGG currently has given it an average game weight rating of 2.3 - also, this is a nearly identical comment to one I left in my "Wurfel Bohnanza" game comments).  All in all, I'd be happy to play this one again (but not with just 2).  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 7.5/10

RATING: 6
I really enjoy playing this game for a variety of reasons.  Visually the game is very appealing with beautiful artwork that evokes a distinctive "feeling" that just seems to fit well with the theme (as does the artwork in Mystery Rummy: Jack the Ripper if that helps explain what I mean).  The rules are simple to understand without sacrificing game play, and the gameplay itself is rather elegant.  I particularly like that you don't have to finish construction of a building yourself to gain rewards.  In fact, clever initial contributions coupled with patience while letting others complete buildings can reward you handsomely.  I also like that completed buildings continue to influence rewards for completion of adjacent buildings.  In addition, the play time seems just about right at 1 hour (once you are completely familiar with the rules and cards).  The only one gripe I have about the game is that a few bad card draws in the end-game can make or break you.  This is a minor gripe, but it can be irritating if you have carefully put yourself in a position to win, only to be shut down by your inability to draw cards of a certain color (or even white cards).  Oh well, there's always next game.

Those dragon scales are more valuable than you realise.

Update: I wonder what the original theme was here, but there's a strong aspect of leeching/working with others and (for the most part for me) finishing their work to either get a small reward for little work or a lot of reward (by doing the 'most' in a location) for a decent amount of work. Rating up from 7 to 7.6.

RATING: 7
I'd say this game has surprising depth, but since it's designed by Reiner Knizia I shouldn't be surprised. Several Knizia signature mechanics interwoven here, perhaps most prominently the aspect of scoring tiles that give players incentives to swarm like sharks when things start to heat up in a particular area. Refusal to glom onto the active spot and contribute to the chain-reaction scoring even while helping others spells trouble for the stubborn holdout. Timing is key here, and while the game looks pretty simple there are numerous things to keep track of, including board position, status of scoring areas, increasing cost of building the obelisk, which cards to possibly discard and, of course, the best use of cards in hand. This, especially, presents tough choices because, while the special powers give your hand of cards some flexibility, it usually comes at the cost of a somewhat inefficient use of resources. Rating unlikely to go down but very well may increase with additional plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

I do like how all of the cards are balanced by the 1's having a better ability, the 2's having some ability, and the 3's having no ability.  The ability to cycle cards is nice, and the possibility for interesting card combinations is there, but most turns are still pretty straightforward.  The game does scale pretty well from 2 to 4 players, although it's a bit too chaotic with more people.

RATING: 7
The cards' pairing of values and abilities is well done, and there's good challenge in trying to get your opponent to want to complete a tile that you've started and will give you good benefits. The game is fun and pretty accessible, but it does suffer from one classic Euro problem: it's overbalanced to the point that it often seems it doesn't really matter what you do, all players will be really close.

I'm impressed by this game's popularity among people I try it with. Almost everyone was a fan after the first play. I think it might be because it allows niceness and cooperation into a competitive game, coupled with a good demonstration of progress (completing buildings, getting resources, all that jazz).

RATING: 8
Blue Moon City - the board game - picks up where the two-player game, (Blue Moon), ended: the reconstruction of the destroyed city of Blue Moon. The board, illustrated by Franz Vohwinkel as well as many well-known American fantasy artists, consists of 21 large building tiles, which show building plans on one side and the buildings in their reconstructed glory on the other. As in the 2-player game, the game includes 3 large molded plastic dragons.

At the start of the game, the board tiles all show their building plan sides. The object of the game is to use cards featuring the races of Blue Moon to help rebuild the city and, at the end, put the large Crystal of the Obelisk in the middle of the city back together. Whenever a building is completely rebuilt, its tile is turned back over to its rebuilt side. The players who helped with a building get crystals and dragon favors, which can be traded in for crystals at certain times.

The player who first manages to add the required number of markers by paying crystals to the Obelisk wins the game. (four markers in a 4-player game, five markers in a 3-player game, and six markers in a 2-player game)

There are two mini-expansions for this game (Blue Moon City: Expansion Tile Sets 1 & 2), each consisting of two tiles that can be added to the main game either separately or combined. The first mini-expansion was included with the Der Knizia Almanach. The second mini-expansion was included in the 6/06 issue of Spielbox.

Original Knizia design--after dragon scales score, ALL players discard scales. This rule was modified by the publisher.

RATING: 7
Played for the first time in 2013, this game has aged ever so slightly. This is reminiscent of a Knizia at the height of his creativity. Many aspects of the game are well balanced, but the ideas are not fresh. The semi-cooperative nature of the game probably felt new in 2006, but not these days where coop games are a dime a dozen. Players need to take advantage and cooperate to rebuild the city structures. Going it alone is fine, but not nearly as efficient as sharing the burden (and rewards) of reconstructions. Luck of the draw in cards and sometimes prevent players from making the desired play, but clever hand management (using the jokers or "hoax" color-switch cards) can overcome that impediment. There are several signature Knizia mechanisms buried within the game. For example, limited cube supply per player(ten) is clever way to slowdown the leaders since cubes placed on the monument to win the game will essentially be out of the game as well. Clever design, just feels a little old.

Sept 2015: Design does feel old but nonetheless still an excellent game. Game play has a semi-coop feel to it though players who strayed away from the group play and set out on their own maybe be left out. It appears with 4 players, staying together in a bunch formation to capitalize on completing the tiles together is the way to go. Otherwise, an excellent design.

RATING: 6
This one's easy to explain: you're some sort of pointy hat dude represented by a neat token that wanders around the fantasy-themed Blue Moon City making contributions to dragons in order to build its buildings and collect crystals to then offer those to an Obelisk (not the cartoon character). Ok? This is essentially a hand-management card game. Random starting tile placement adds some replay value. Has a "Gateway" difficulty level. An absolutely gorgeous game in terms of art and components. Plays well enough but the theme doesn't really come through for me, making the game mostly feel like its mechanisms, and seriously diminishing the experience in my view. RATING: 7
Al and I each had five offerings on the Washington Monument. Whoever could get 12 crystals and get to the center first would win. Al had 11 crystals and was four spaces (two turns) from the center and two spaces (one turn) from my position. I had 12 crystals and was two spaces (one turn) from the center. It was Al's turn. He turned to me and said, "Clearly, you are 'going up' next turn. I have no choice. I must now land on you, kill you, and take a crystal. Where are the dice?"

For some inexplicable reason, Blue Moon City triggers a Talisman sense memory. Maybe it is that it is a race game where we run around, picking cards and hope for the best, and accumulate dragon scales, like dead enemies, for points.

RATING: 8
When Knizia's on, he's ON.  This game has great agonizing decisions, and a lot of really neat optimization.  You constantly have to decide whether to play your cards for their numerical strength, or for their special ability.  Based on the situation, the utility of the cards in your hand can go up or down.  It's tempting to go for just a few more crystals, but you're going to be in rough shape if somebody else makes their donations before you.  I have some concerns after a healthy number of plays that there is a pretty strong first-player bias, and that luck and player chaos can play a very strong role, but for a game like this that is so bite-sized, I'm willing to forgive a lot.

RATING: 9
UPDATE: Increased rating from 8.5 to 9. May rise to 10 in time. A beautiful-looking, evergreen game with tactical depth. It improves with repeat plays.

Players move around Blue Moon City, contributing to buildings by playing groups of coloured cards. Finishing buildings gives you crystals. You can also gain crystals by collecting dragon scales, which you get by attracting dragons when you contribute to buildings.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Not a bad game, but I thought I'd like it more than I did. It reminded me a lot of Kingdoms - not mechanically, but in terms of what you're trying to accomplish (that is, setting yourself up for a good combo without it being so good that your opponents won't take it from you before your next turn). The difference (besides that this is a much more subtle game of small advantages, which I don't want to downplay) is that, in Kingdoms, you know everything that's out there, and that just about everything will come into play during any given round. In Blue Moon City, the randomness of the cards (and size of the deck) means you have no idea whether or not anyone can do anything on their turn, unless you're an excellent card counter. This makes long term strategy fairly difficult, which in turn minimizes the main tension in the game for me (using the cards for their powers or holding on for their values). I should note I've only played this with 2, but I don't know if more players would add more balance or chaos. Ditto with including the expansion tiles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 6
Another overwrought FFG product (though Kosmos bears at least as much blame).  Could [i]easily[/i] fit into the thicker Silver Line box (Through the Desert), or one even smaller than that.

Gameplay is pretty simple, but strategies and tactics seem difficult, at least at first. But further play shows it's on rails, with finishes artificially close.

Seems  very easy to fall into a "group vs. one" pattern, where trying to (or being forced to) go it alone will fail badly.  Reminded me strongly of Tower of Babel in terms of directing points to opponents in order to advance your own cause, and yet I didn't like it as much as ToB as I felt more at their mercy than able to chart my own course.

RATING: 7
purchased 04.07.07 played 2 times previously. Yet another Knizia that I have grown to love *much* more with repeated plays. What we have is a light-medium weight game which plays quickly, is fun to play with gamers and family. After 6 plays, I'm still sticking to an 8 rating. Is it possible to go for really trying to drag as many dragons as you can at each contribution phase to cycle through the scales as often as possible? I dunno, but another strategy worth considering. So far it always seems a good thing to ride the backs of other players rather than going it alone. Interesting game.

RATING: 8
Great components, great strategy, nice use of the Blue Moon setting and established art/motif. I liked the number and type of decisions, and the fact that coming in second could be almost as beneficial as coming in first -- the rewards for participating in the scoring was tempting enough to warrant a lot of careful decision making, especially at the risk of letting someone resolve a region by himself and take all the credit solo. After 5+ Plays: I didn't like it so much with two players.  Helping your only opponent is counterproductive, so you basically finish each tile yourself.  This dissuaded me from buying the game, but I'll still happily play it.
